Magians and Mu'tazila: A Comparison on Divine Power
Comparison of Magians and Mu'tazila on limiting divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author compares the Magians and Mu'tazila on the issue of divine power and argues that the Mu'tazila are more misguided.

Core Idea
Limiting God's power leads to associating partners with Him—the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians.

In this passage, the author draws a striking comparison between two groups that restrict God's power to good only: the Magians and the Mu'tazila. While both err, he argues that the Mu'tazila commit a greater error by multiplying partners for God. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the parallel structure signaled by 'وكذا' (likewise).
Pay attention to how the author repeats the same pattern for both groups.
2
Identify the shared error
Both groups limit God's power to good only and deny His power over evil. They also attribute creation to others besides God.
The author uses 'وكذا' to show agreement on these points.
3
Spot the contrast
The author then distinguishes them: Magians affirm only one partner for God, while Mu'tazila affirm countless partners. Additionally, Magians attribute all good to God's power, whereas Mu'tazila attribute the goodness of good things to others.
The phrase 'بل المجوس أسعد حالاً' (rather the Magians are more fortunate) signals a contrast.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the author considers the Mu'tazila worse. How does limiting God's power lead to multiplying partners? This ties to the earlier discussion on the creation of acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجوس جعلت العالم بالله وبغيره ولا قدرة له على ما هو لغيره ولا قدرة لذلك الغير على ما هو له، وكذا المعتزلة جعلوا بالله وبغيره من الخالقين ولا قدرة له على ما هو لغيره ولا قدرة لغيره على ما هو له، وكذا المجوس تأبى دخول ما هو قبيح تحت قدرة الله تعالى وقصرت قدرته على المحاسن، وكذا المعتزلة ساعدوهم على هذا الأصل، وبنوا عليه جميع مذاهبهم، بل المجوس أسعد حالاً، لأنهم ما أثبتوا العالم إلا لله تعالى ولآخر، فاقتصروا على إثبات شريك واحد له، والمعتزلة أثبتوا له ما لا يحصون من الشركاء، وكذا أولئك أدخلوا جميع المحاسن تحت قدرته والمعتزلة أضافوا حسن المحاسن إلى غيره ولم يثبتوا الله تعالى على مثل تلك المحاسن قدرة على ما قررنا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، وإذا عرف هذا ظهر المراد بالاسم، والله الموفق.
The Magians made the world belong to God and to another, and [said] He has no power over what belongs to another, and that other has no power over what belongs to Him. Likewise, the Mu'tazila made [the world] belong to God and to other creators, and He has no power over what belongs to another, nor does another have power over what belongs to Him. Likewise, the Magians refuse to admit what is evil under the power of God Most High and limited His power to good things. Likewise, the Mu'tazila assisted them in this principle and built all their doctrines upon it. Rather, the Magians are more fortunate, because they only affirmed the world as belonging to God Most High and to another, so they confined themselves to affirming one partner for Him, while the Mu'tazila affirmed for Him partners beyond count. Likewise, those [Magians] included all good things under His power, while the Mu'tazila attributed the goodness of good things to other than Him and did not affirm for God Most High power over such good things, as we have established in the issue of the creation of acts. When this is known, the meaning of the name becomes clear. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage compares Magians and Mu'tazila on the issue of divine power and the attribution of good and evil. The author argues that both groups limit God's power, but the Mu'tazila are worse. He states that Magians made the world belong to God and another, each with exclusive power over their own domain. Mu'tazila similarly make other creators alongside God, denying God power over what is not His and vice versa. Both groups refuse to admit evil under God's power, restricting it to good. However, Magians only affirm one partner, while Mu'tazila affirm countless partners. Additionally, Magians attribute all good to God's power, whereas Mu'tazila attribute the goodness of good things to others, denying God power over them. The author references his earlier discussion on the creation of acts. He concludes that understanding this clarifies the intended meaning of the 'name' (likely the term 'Mu'tazila' or a related designation).

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Limiting God's power is a minor error.
Correction: It leads to associating partners with God, as shown by the Mu'tazila's multiplication of partners.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how do the Mu'tazila compare to the Magians in the number of partners they affirm for God?
A) The Mu'tazila affirm fewer partners than the Magians.
B) The Magians affirm countless partners, while the Mu'tazila affirm only one.
C) The Mu'tazila affirm more partners than the Magians.
D) Both affirm the same number of partners.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are more misguided than the Magians because they affirm countless partners for God while restricting His power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your heart, limit God's power by assuming He cannot bring good out of what you perceive as evil? What would it mean for you to truly believe that nothing falls outside His power, even the things you hate?
?
The Mu'tazila attributed the goodness of their own actions to themselves, not to God. When you do something good, do you silently take credit for it in your heart, or do you see it as a gift from God alone?
?
If you truly believed that limiting God's power is a form of associating partners with Him, how would you change the way you think about your own choices and the evil you see in the world?

The Attitude of the Mu'tazila and the Sunnis towards Allah's Decree
The author uses a hadith on qadr to contrast Sunni submission with Mu'tazila disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the hadith on qadr to argue that the Mu'tazila, not the Sunnis, are the true opponents of Allah because they dispute His justice and demand rights from Him.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila dispute with Allah; the Sunnis submit to His decree.

In this passage, the author presents a stark contrast between the attitude of the Sunnis and that of the Mu'tazila towards Allah's decree and justice. He uses a hadith on qadr as a foundation to argue that the Mu'tazila, by demanding that Allah must do what is best for them and reward them as a right, are the ones who truly oppose Allah. The Sunnis, on the other hand, submit to His will without dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its significance
Notice the hadith that qadr is from Allah and that it is a condition of faith. Understand that the author uses it to link the Mu'tazila with the Magians who deny it.
The hadith establishes the foundation for the argument.
2
Examine the Sunni attitude
Identify how the Sunnis respond to Allah's actions – they do not dispute punishment, they do not demand reward, and they bear calamities with patience.
Their submission reflects true faith in Allah's wisdom and justice.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila attitude
See how the Mu'tazila dispute with Allah, demanding what is most beneficial and insisting on reward as a right, implying that Allah's lordship depends on fulfilling their rights.
This attitude is contrasted sharply with the Sunni submission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيد هذا ما رواه غير واحد عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم 'القدر خيره وشره من الله تعالى'. وروى أنه جعل هذا من شرط الإيمان والمجوس يكذبونه في ذلك وساعدت المعتزلة المجوس على تكذيب النبي عليه السلام في ذلك، وما روت المعتزلة من الحديث من أدل الدلائل على أن الاسم واقع عليهم دون خصمائهم فإن خصماءهم وإن قالوا إن الله تعالى هو الخالق لأفعالهم إلا أنهم لا يخاصمونه في التعذيب على ما قبح منها بل يرون ذلك حكمة وعدلاً، وكذا تخليقه على ما مر قبل هذا، وكذا لا يطالبونه بثواب ما أتوا به من الطاعات ويقولون ما أدينا إلا حقك والمحن والمصائب قابلوا ذلك منه بالصبر وطلبوا منه إزالة ذلك بفضله وكرمه ولا يطالبون على ذلك منه عوضاً إقراراً منهم أن أنفسهم وأموالهم له يتصرف بذلك كله في ملكه يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد لا يسأل عما يفعل وهم يسألون، فأما المعتزلة فهم الذين يخاصمونه ابتداء وانتهاء، فيقولون له ليس لك أن تفعل بنا إلا ما هو الأصلح لنا، وأن مصالحنا واجب عليك إعطاؤها وهي حق لنا مستحق قبلك إن لم تؤدها إلينا صرت ظالمًا جائراً ، سفيها مانعا حقا عليك ولا بقاء لك على ربوبيتك وسلطانك إلا بأداء ما هو حقنا قبلك، وإذا أطعناك بشيء مما أمرت وأدينا شكر بعض ما أوفيتنا من حقوقنا الواجبة عليك وجبت عليك مكافأتنا على ذلك ومقابلتنا بالإثابة على ما أدينا من حقك قبلنا لا يسعك إلا الخروج عن ذلك، وليس لك أن تؤلمنا من غير سابقة جناية إلا بشرط أن تعوضنا عن ذلك كما هو على كل من يتعرض ملك غيره ويتجاوز تصرفه عما هو مملوك له، ثم من عرف المذهبين وجعل الفريق الأول خصماء الله تعالى دون الفريق الثاني فهو مكابر معاند.
This is supported by what more than one transmitted from the Prophet ﷺ: 'Qadr, its good and its evil, is from Allah Most High.' And it was narrated that he made this a condition of faith, while the Magians deny him in that, and the Mu'tazila assisted the Magians in denying the Prophet عليه السلام in that. What the Mu'tazila have narrated of hadith is among the clearest evidences that the name applies to them, not to their opponents. For their opponents, even though they say that Allah Most High is the Creator of their acts, yet they do not dispute with Him regarding punishment for what is evil among them, but rather consider that wisdom and justice. Likewise, His creation as mentioned before. Likewise, they do not demand from Him reward for what they perform of acts of obedience, and they say: 'We have only fulfilled Your right.' As for calamities and afflictions, they meet them with patience and ask Him to remove them by His bounty and generosity, and they do not demand compensation from Him for that, acknowledging that their selves and their wealth belong to Him, and He disposes of all that in His kingdom, doing what He wills and judging what He desires. He is not asked about what He does, while they are asked. As for the Mu'tazila, they are the ones who dispute with Him from beginning to end, saying to Him: 'You may not do to us except what is most beneficial for us, and our benefits are obligatory upon You to give, and they are a right we deserve from You. If You do not deliver them to us, You become an oppressor, unjust, foolish, preventing a right due from You. There is no survival for Your lordship and authority except by fulfilling what is our right from You. And when we obey You in something You command and fulfill gratitude for some of the rights You have given us, it becomes obligatory upon You to reward us for that and to requite us with recompense for what we have fulfilled of Your right from us. You have no choice but to do that. And You may not cause us pain without prior wrongdoing except on condition that You compensate us for it, just as it is for anyone who encroaches on another's property and exceeds his disposal over what is owned by him.' Then, whoever knows the two schools and makes the first group the opponents of Allah Most High rather than the second group is a stubborn obstinate person.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a proof and contrast. The author supports the previous argument with a hadith that qadr (divine decree), both good and evil, is from Allah, and that belief in it is a condition of faith. The Magians deny this, and the Mu'tazila assist them in denying the Prophet. The author then argues that the hadith transmitted by the Mu'tazila themselves prove that the name 'opponents of Allah' applies to them, not to the Sunnis. He contrasts the two groups: The Sunnis (the opponents of the Mu'tazila) affirm that Allah creates their acts but do not dispute with Him over punishment for evil acts; they see it as wisdom and justice. They do not demand reward for obedience, acknowledging they only fulfill Allah's right. They bear calamities with patience and ask for removal through Allah's bounty, not demanding compensation, recognizing that everything belongs to Him. In contrast, the Mu'tazila dispute with Allah from start to end. They demand that Allah must do what is most beneficial for them, that their benefits are obligatory upon Him, and if He does not give them, He becomes unjust. They insist that His lordship depends on fulfilling their rights. When they obey, they demand reward as a right. They also demand compensation for any pain not preceded by wrongdoing. The author concludes that anyone who knows both schools and calls the Sunnis opponents of Allah rather than the Mu'tazila is obstinate.

Key Terms
القدر
الخصماء
المعتزلة
أهل السنة
الصلح
الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God's control over evil leads to a more reverent attitude towards God.
Correction: The author shows that denying God's control over evil (as the Mu'tazila do) actually leads to disputing with God and demanding rights from Him, which is the opposite of reverence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the attitude of the Mu'tazila towards God regarding reward and punishment?
A) They accept God's decree with patience and do not demand reward.
B) They consider reward and punishment as a right they can demand from God.
C) They deny that God has any role in their actions.
D) They believe God is unjust if He punishes them.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila, by demanding rights from Allah, become His opponents, while the Sunnis submit to His decree without dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'Why me? I don't deserve this'? How does that thought mirror the Mu'tazila demand for compensation, and what would it mean for you to respond instead with the Sunni attitude of patience and seeking Allah's bounty?
?
In your acts of worship, do you ever feel that Allah owes you reward because of your efforts? How does the Sunni confession 'We have only fulfilled Your right' challenge that feeling, and what would change in your relationship with Allah if you truly internalized that?
?
Have you ever demanded from Allah that He give you something you consider 'best' for you, as if He were obligated to fulfill your expectations? How does the Mu'tazila's claim 'You may not do to us except what is most beneficial' expose the subtle entitlement in your heart, and what step can you take today to surrender that demand?

Refutation of the Claim of Resembling Magians
The author refutes the charge that Sunnis agree with Magians on attributing marriage of daughters to Allah's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that Sunnis resemble Magians by distinguishing between attributing an act to Allah based on its goodness vs. based on its occurrence.

Core Idea
Superficial agreement on attribution does not equal true agreement when the underlying reasons differ.

You may have heard accusations that Sunnis share a problematic view with Magians regarding certain actions. In this passage, the author of the Tabṣirah directly addresses such a charge. He argues that the similarity is only apparent, and by analyzing the logic behind each group's position, he demonstrates a fundamental disagreement on the principle of attribution to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's tone and the claim he refutes.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the Magian position
Identify why Magians attribute the marriage of daughters to Allah. The author says they do so based on their false assumption that it is good and permitted.
The reason is moral goodness, not occurrence.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila position
See how Magians and Mu'tazila actually agree in their reason for attribution. Both base it on goodness, though they differ in applying it.
This is a key point of the refutation.
4
Grasp the author's key insight
The author highlights that the reason (علة) for attribution determines true agreement. Sunnis attribute based on occurrence, not goodness.
Outward similarity does not imply real agreement.
5
Conclude why the charge fails
Appreciate that outward assistance with difference in reason does not count as agreement, so the accusation of resemblance is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعموا من مساعدتنا المجوس بقولنا: إن نكاح البنات بإرادة الله تعالى تمويه محض، فإنهم يضيفون ذلك إلى الله تعالى بناء على ظنهم الكاذب أن ذلك مشروع وهو حسن ولو علموا قبحه لساعدوا المعتزلة في نفي إرادة الله تعالى عن ذلك، ألا ترى أنك لو سألتهم عن علة إضافتهم ذلك إلى إرادة الله تعالى اعتلوا بكونه حسنًا لا بكونه محدثًا ، فإذا هم مع المعتزلة في علة جواز الإضافة والمنع، وخالفونا أشد المخالفة كما خالفتنا المعتزلة، إلا أنهم ظنا منهم أن ذلك حسن ساعدونا في الظاهر والمساعدة في الظاهر مع الاختلاف في العلة لن يعد موافقة.
And what they claim of our assisting the Magians by our saying that the marriage of daughters is by the will of Allah Most High is pure obfuscation. For they [the Magians] attribute that to Allah Most High based on their false assumption that it is permitted and good. If they knew its evil, they would assist the Mu'tazila in denying the will of Allah Most High regarding it. Do you not see that if you asked them about the reason for their attributing that to the will of Allah Most High, they would give as reason that it is good, not that it is originated. Thus, they are with the Mu'tazila regarding the reason for permissibility of attribution and prohibition, and they oppose us most strongly just as the Mu'tazila opposed us. However, because they assumed that is good, they outwardly assisted us. But outward assistance with difference in reason does not count as agreement.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Sunnis resemble Magians by attributing the marriage of daughters to Allah's will. The author states this claim is pure obfuscation. He explains that Magians attribute such actions to Allah based on their false assumption that these actions are permissible and good. If they knew they were evil, they would side with the Mu'tazila in denying Allah's will regarding them. The key point is the reason for attribution: Magians attribute because they consider the act good, not because it occurs. In this reason, they agree with the Mu'tazila (who also consider goodness as the basis for attribution, though they deny evil acts are from Allah). Thus, Magians and Mu'tazila are actually aligned on the principle of attribution based on goodness, while Sunnis base attribution on occurrence (all things are by Allah's will). Therefore, the apparent similarity between Sunnis and Magians is superficial; the underlying reason differs, so it is not true agreement.

Key Terms
نكاح البنات
إرادة الله
الحسن
القبيح
الإضافة
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing an act to Allah's will implies that Allah approves of it morally.
Correction: The author shows that Magians attribute based on perceived goodness, but Sunnis attribute based on occurrence alone, without moral judgment.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis for the Magians' attribution of the marriage of daughters to Allah's will?
A) They believe it is an originated act.
B) They believe it is good and permissible.
C) They believe it is evil but still from Allah.
D) They believe all acts are from Allah regardless of goodness.

Key Takeaway
The difference between Sunnis and Magians is not in the fact of attribution but in the reason for it — Magians attribute based on perceived goodness, while Sunnis attribute all events to Allah's will regardless of moral evaluation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'everything happens by Allah's will,' do you sometimes use that phrase to justify actions you know are wrong? Examine your heart: do you attribute based on your desire to see something as good, or do you truly submit to Allah's decree regardless of your moral judgment? How will you change your understanding today?
?
The Magians attributed an evil act to Allah because they thought it was good. Are there any beliefs or actions you hold onto because you've convinced yourself they are good, even though the Qur'an and Sunnah clearly indicate otherwise? What would it take for you to re-evaluate and align with the truth?
?
This lesson shows that outward agreement is not true agreement if the underlying reason differs. In your own life, are there areas where you outwardly agree with Islamic teachings but inwardly your reasoning is closer to other ideologies or desires? Commit to aligning not just your words but your reasoning with the Sunnah.

Refuting the Mu'tazila Doubt on Life and Body
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila's doubt that life requires physical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's argument that life requires a physical structure is refuted by Nasafi, and how it connects to philosophical arguments about God.

Core Idea
Life is an attribute subsisting in a self-subsistent entity, not in a physical structure.

The Mu'tazila raised a doubt regarding the punishment of the grave: knowledge requires life, life requires a physical structure, and in the grave the body disintegrates. Nasafi refutes this by denying that structure is a condition for life, and traces the origin of this error to the philosophers who deny God's attributes. Let's examine the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's doubt
First, read the passage to see the exact formulation of the Mu'tazila argument: knowledge needs life, life needs structure, and in scattered atoms there is no structure. Understand their chain of reasoning.
Notice how they extend this to deny pain and pleasure in the grave.
2
Identify Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi dismisses the doubt as 'weak' and explains that the only condition for an attribute to subsist in a subject is that the subject is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi al-dhāt), not that it has a physical structure.
This is a key point about the nature of attributes.
3
Trace the origin to philosophers
Nasafi reveals that the Mu'tazila borrowed this idea from the muʿaṭṭila (deniers) among the philosophers, who claim that structure is necessary for life and thus deny life to God since He has no structure.
The Mu'tazila did not dare apply it to God but applied it to the dead.
4
Reflect on the deeper implication
Consider why Nasafi traces the argument to its source: the doubt about life and structure is not just about the grave but ultimately aims to deny God's attributes. The refutation protects both the reality of divine attributes and the possibility of punishment in the grave.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة للمعتزلة والرد عليها : وشبهة المعتزلة أن العلم لا وجود له بدون الحياة، وأن الحياة لا تصور لها بدون البنية، وفي الأجزاء المتفرقة الصائرة أجزاء غير متجزئة لا بنية لها فلا يتصور فيها الحياة فلا يتصور العلم ولا التألم والتلذذ. شبهة واهية إذ كون البنية شرطًا للحياة ممنوع ولا شرط لصحة قيام الصفة به بمحل إلا القيام بالذات، وإنما هذا شيء تلقنته المعتزلة من المعطلة من جملة الفلاسفة حيث يزعمون أن البنية المخصوصة شرط لكون الذات حيًا، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه البنية فيستحيل أن يكون حيًا فتلقنت منهم المعتزلة ذلك، ولم يتجاسروا على إثبات ذلك صريحًا فأثبتت ما يؤدي إليه، فزعمت أن لا تصور للحياة بدون البنية علما منهم
A doubt of the Mu'tazila and the response to it: Their doubt is that knowledge has no existence without life, and life has no conception without physical structure. In the scattered parts that become indivisible atoms, there is no structure, so life cannot be conceived in them, and thus neither knowledge nor pain and pleasure can be conceived. This is a weak doubt, because the claim that physical structure is a condition for life is denied; rather, the only condition for the valid subsistence of an attribute in a subject is that the subject subsist by itself (al-qiyām bi al-dhāt). This is something the Mu'tazila learned from the deniers (al-muʿaṭṭila) among the philosophers, who claim that a specific structure is a condition for a subject to be alive, and since God Almighty cannot have a structure, He would be impossible to be alive. The Mu'tazila learned that from them, and they did not dare to explicitly affirm it, so they affirmed what leads to it, claiming that life cannot be conceived without structure, knowing that...
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents a specific doubt (shubha) of the Mu'tazila regarding the impossibility of punishment of the grave, and the author's refutation. The Mu'tazila argue: knowledge cannot exist without life, and life cannot be conceived without a physical structure (al-binya). After death, the body decomposes into scattered parts (indivisible atoms) that lack structure, so life cannot reside in them, consequently knowledge, pain, and pleasure are impossible. The author dismisses this as a 'weak doubt' (shubha wāhiya). He refutes it by denying that a physical structure is a condition for life. The only requirement for an attribute (such as life) to subsist in a subject (maḥall) is that the subject be a self-subsistent entity (qāʾim bi al-dhāt) — it does not need a body. He then traces the origin of this error: the Mu'tazila borrowed it from the muʿaṭṭila (a term for those who deny God's attributes, often associated with philosophers). The philosophers claim that a specific structure is necessary for a subject to be alive, and since God cannot have a body, they would conclude God is not alive. The Mu'tazila did not dare to openly adopt this consequence for God, but they applied the same reasoning to deny life to the dead in the grave. Thus the author exposes the hidden philosophical source of their argument.

Key Terms
شبهة
بنية
قيام بالذات
معطلة
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life requires a physical body or structure to exist.
Correction: Life is an attribute that can subsist in any self-subsistent entity, not necessarily a physical structure. The only condition is that the entity is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi al-dhāt).

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what do the philosophers falsely claim is a condition for a subject to be alive?
A) A soul
B) A specific physical structure
C) Free will
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's doubt that life requires a physical structure is refuted because the only condition for an attribute to subsist is self-subsistence, and this doubt is traced back to philosophers who deny that God is alive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila unknowingly built their argument on a premise borrowed from philosophers who denied God's life. What assumptions do you hold about your faith that might come from sources you haven't examined? Commit to tracing one belief back to its origin this week—who taught you it, and is it from revelation or something else?
?
If the only condition for an attribute to subsist is self-subsistence, then your own soul after death can still know, feel pain, and experience pleasure without a physical body. Are you living today as if that moment is real and imminent? Write down one concrete change you will make this week because the grave is not an end but a beginning of a more intense life.
?
The author exposes the hidden philosophical roots of a theological error. Are there ideas you accept in your daily life (from culture, media, or even well-meaning teachers) that contradict Islamic teachings? Name one such idea and resolve to learn the Islamic counter-evidence from the sources by the end of this month.

Three Views on the Fate of Grave Sinners
The author presents the positions of Ahl al-Haqq and Mu'tazila on the naming and final fate of grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major Islamic theological positions on the naming and final judgment of grave sinners: the moderate view of Ahl al-Haqq, the extreme Murji'a view, and the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
Grave sinners remain believers but are subject to God's will — neither beyond forgiveness nor beyond punishment.

Imagine a Muslim commits a major sin. Is he still a believer? Will he be punished eternally? Different Islamic schools have given distinct answers. In this passage, the author lays out the question, then presents the positions of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth), the extreme Murji'a, and the Mu'tazila. Understanding these differences is crucial for grasping early Islamic theological debates about faith, sin, and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and overview
Read the opening lines where the author states the issue: by what name should grave sinners be called, and what is their judgment in the afterlife? Notice that the discussion includes people of the qibla (all Muslims in prayer direction).
This frames the entire debate.
2
Understand the Ahl al-Haqq position
Read the first view, attributed to Ahl al-Haqq. Note the conditions: the sinner did not consider the sin lawful or treat it lightly, but acted out of desire or zeal. This person remains a believer, and his final outcome is Paradise, though he may be punished temporarily if God wills. Also note the emphasis on hope and fear, not certainty of forgiveness.
This view is associated with Abu Hanifa and early Sunni theology.
3
Distinguish the Murji'a position
The author then clarifies that Abu Hanifa was called a Murji' because he deferred judgment to God, but the 'wicked Murji'a' hold an extreme view: no Muslim will be punished for any major sin, and sin does not harm faith. This is a distinct view that the author rejects. Read the short rebuttal or distinction.
Be careful not to conflate the two positions.
4
Examine the Mu'tazila position
Finally, read the Mu'tazila view: the grave sinner is called a fasiq, occupying a station between faith and disbelief. If he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally in the Fire, and his faith will not benefit him. This contrasts sharply with Ahl al-Haqq.
The Mu'tazila emphasized divine justice and human free will.
5
Compare and contrast
Now that you have all three views, create a mental or written comparison. Focus on the name given (believer, fasiq, etc.), the role of repentance, and the final outcome. Reflect on why the author presents these views in this order.
This will solidify your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأسماء والأحكام والوعد والوعيد اختلف الناس في مقترفي الكبائر من أهل القبلة أنهم بماذا يسمون وأن حكمهم في الآخرة ما هو؟ قال أهل الحق: إن من اقترف كبيرة غير مستحل لها ولا مستخف بها لغلبة شهوة أو حمية يرجو الله تعالى أن يغفر له ويخاف أن يعذبه فهذا اسمه مؤمن، بقي على ما كان عليه من الإيمان لم يزل عنه إيمانه ولم ينتقض، ولا يخرج أحد الإيمان إلا من الباب الذي دخل فيه، فحكمه أنه لو مات من غير توبة فلله فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه بفضله وكرمه أو يتركه وما معه من الإيمان والحسنات أو بشفاعة بعض الأخيار، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة ولا يخلد في النار، وكان أبو حنيفة - رحمه الله يسمى مرجئًا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، والإرجاء هو التأخير، وروى عنه أنه قيل له ممن أخذت الإرجاء، فقال: من الملائكة عليهم السلام حيث قالوا: لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا وزعمت المرجئة الخبيثة أن أحدًا من المسلمين لا يعاقب على شيء من الكبائر، وكما أن الحسنة لا تنفع مع الكفر فالسيئة لا تضر مع الإيمان حكى هذا القول عن مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير. وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر ، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه
Discussion of names, judgments, promise, and threat. People differed concerning those who commit major sins among the people of the qibla: by what are they called, and what is their judgment in the afterlife? The people of truth said: Whoever commits a major sin without considering it lawful and without treating it lightly, due to overwhelming desire or zeal, hoping that God Almighty will forgive him and fearing that He will punish him – this one's name is believer; he remains upon what he was of faith; his faith has not left him nor has it been nullified. And no one leaves faith except through the door by which he entered. His judgment is that if he dies without repentance, then it is up to God: if He wills, He pardons him by His grace and generosity, or He leaves him with what he has of faith and good deeds, or through the intercession of some righteous ones; and if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin, then his ultimate end is Paradise necessarily, and he will not abide eternally in the Fire. Abu Hanifa – may God have mercy on him – was called a Murji' because he deferred the matter of the grave sinner to the will of God Almighty. 'Irja' means deferment. It is narrated from him that it was said to him: From whom did you take irja? He said: From the angels, peace be upon them, when they said: 'We have no knowledge except what You taught us.' The wicked Murji'a claimed that none of the Muslims will be punished for any major sin, and just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief, so a sin does not harm with faith. This saying is reported from Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the commentator. The Mu'tazila said: The name of this one is 'fasiq' (sinner); he is called neither believer nor disbeliever, and he has a station between the two stations of faith and disbelief. His judgment is that if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire and his faith will not benefit him.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a comparative exposition of the views of different sects regarding the naming and judgment of grave sinners (those who commit major sins) from among the people of the qibla. The author first states the question: what is the name and judgment of such a person in the afterlife? He then presents the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth): such a person remains a believer; he has not lost his faith. If he dies without repentance, God may pardon him, or punish him according to his sin, and his final destination is Paradise; he will not be eternally in the Fire. The author notes that Abu Hanifa was called a Murji' because he deferred the judgment of the grave sinner to God's will (irja' meaning deferment). Abu Hanifa is said to have derived irja' from the angels' statement in the Qur'an. Then the author distinguishes the position of Ahl al-Haqq from that of the 'wicked Murji'a', who claim that no Muslim will be punished for any major sin, and that sin does not harm faith just as disbelief nullifies good deeds. This extreme view is attributed to Muqatil ibn Sulayman. Finally, the author presents the Mu'tazila position: the grave sinner is called fasiq, occupying a station between faith and disbelief (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). If he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally in the Fire, and his faith will not benefit him.

Key Terms
كبيرة
مؤمن
فاسق
منزلة بين منزلتين
إرجاء
مرجئة
معتزلة
أهل الحق
أبو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Murji'as believe that sin does not harm faith and no Muslim will be punished.
Correction: The author distinguishes between Abu Hanifa, who deferred judgment to God (hence called Murji'), and the 'wicked Murji'a' who claimed absolute impunity. Ahl al-Haqq's view is that the grave sinner may be punished temporarily.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the final outcome of a grave sinner who dies without repentance?
A) He is eternally in the Fire.
B) He is in a station between faith and disbelief.
C) His ultimate end is Paradise, though he may be punished first.
D) He is considered a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
The fate of grave sinners remains in God's hands; Ahl al-Haqq affirm faith and hope, while rejecting both extreme leniency and extreme severity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have committed a major sin. Which of these three views do you truly hope is correct? If you find yourself leaning toward the wicked Murji'a position, what does that reveal about your view of God's justice and your own accountability? Can you honestly say you fear God's punishment for your sins, or do you assume forgiveness without cost?
?
The Mu'tazila teach that your faith cannot save you if you die on a major sin. Does this belief make you more careful about your actions, or does it lead you to despair? How does the Sunni position of hope balanced with fear affect your daily choices?
?
Abu Hanifa deferred the matter to God's will, learning from the angels who said 'We have no knowledge except what You taught us.' In your own life, where are you trying to know what God has not revealed? Can you surrender the judgment of others to God alone, especially those whose sins you find easy to condemn?

The Mu'tazila Position on Major and Minor Sinners
Nasafi outlines the Mu'tazila position on the major sinner's intermediate station and the minor sinner's status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine about the major sinner's intermediate station and the minor sinner's status as a believer.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila placed the major sinner in a middle station — neither believer nor disbeliever.

Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila view that a person who commits a major sin is not a believer but also not a disbeliever; rather, he is a transgressor (fasiq) in an intermediate station. If he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally punished, and his former faith does not save him. In contrast, a minor sinner remains a believer and merits forgiveness if he avoids major sins. This passage clarifies a key Mu'tazila doctrine that shaped classical debates on faith and sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage discusses two distinct cases: the major sinner and the minor sinner.
The passage is concise; each case is stated in one sentence.
2
Identify the terms for each case
For the major sinner, note the term 'fasiq' and the phrase 'manzila bayna al-manzilatayn'. For the minor sinner, note that he is still called a believer.
These terms are key to the Mu'tazila position.
3
Compare the rulings
The major sinner if unrepentant faces eternal punishment; the minor sinner deserves forgiveness if he avoids major sins. Contrast the consequences.
This shows the Mu'tazila's strict view on major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر ، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه وطاعته وليس من الحكمة عفوه ومغفرته، وأما صاحب الصغيرة فهو عندهم مؤمن ولو اجتنب الكبائر لاستحق مغفرة الصغائر ولا يجوز الله تعذيبه عليها.
And the Mu'tazila said: Indeed, the name of this one is a transgressor (fasiq); he is not called a believer nor a disbeliever, and he has a station between the two stations of faith and disbelief. His ruling is that if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith and obedience will not benefit him, and it is not from wisdom to forgive him and pardon him. As for the one who commits a minor sin, he is in their view a believer, and if he avoids major sins, he deserves forgiveness for minor sins, and God may not punish him for them.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine regarding the person who commits a major sin. The Mu'tazila assign such a person the name 'fasiq' (transgressor) rather than believer or disbeliever, placing him in an intermediate station ('manzila bayna al-manzilatayn') between faith and disbelief. His ruling: if he dies without repentance, he will dwell eternally in Hell; his faith and obedience while alive will not benefit him, and it is not in accord with divine wisdom to forgive him. The passage then contrasts the case of the one who commits a minor sin: in the Mu'tazila view, such a person remains a believer, and if he avoids major sins, he merits forgiveness for minor sins, and God cannot justly punish him for them.

Key Terms
فاسق
منزلة بين منزلتين
خلود
صغيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila considered the major sinner a disbeliever.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually placed him in a distinct intermediate category, not disbelief.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila ruling on a person who dies unrepentant after committing a major sin?
A) He is a believer and will be forgiven.
B) He is a disbeliever and will be punished eternally.
C) He is a fasiq in a middle station and will abide eternally in the Fire.
D) He will be punished for a time and then enter Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila distinguished between major and minor sinners: major sinners occupy an intermediate station and face eternal punishment if unrepentant, while minor sinners remain believers and merit forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat minor sins as trivial, assuming you will be forgiven, while ignoring that the Mu'tazila warn even minor sins deserve punishment unless you avoid major ones? What does your daily attitude reveal about your view of God's justice?
?
If you were to die tonight after committing a major sin without repenting, would you expect your previous good deeds and faith to save you? The Mu'tazila say no. Does that thought make you rush to repentance now, or do you rely on a mercy you haven't earned?
?
The Mu'tazila claim it is not from wisdom for God to forgive the unrepentant major sinner. Do you ever assume God will forgive you no matter what, cheapening His justice? How does this teaching challenge your understanding of His attributes?

The Khawarij View: Excommunication for Any Sin
Nasafi presents the Khawarij view on the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij position that any sin constitutes disbelief and eternal punishment, and the internal distinctions they made.

Core Idea
For the Khawarij, a single sin removes one from faith entirely.

The Khawarij were known for their extreme position on the major sinner. Nasafi reports their majority view and some variations within the sect. This passage establishes the key teaching that any act of disobedience, whether minor or major, makes one a disbeliever destined for eternal hellfire.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the majority view.
The passage begins with the consensus of most Khawarij: anyone who disobeys God becomes a disbeliever and will remain in Hell forever.
Notice that they make no distinction between minor and major sins.
2
Note the two variations within the Khawarij.
Some Khawarij say the sinner is a polytheist (mushrik), while others distinguish between minor and major sins, only excommunicating for major sins.
This shows that not all Khawarij held exactly the same doctrine.
3
Reflect on the theological implications.
Consider how this view contrasts with mainstream Sunni theology, which holds that sins do not necessarily remove one from faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول جمهور الخوارج: إن من عصى الله تعالى فقد كفر فكان كافرًا وحكمه أنه يخلد في النار صغيرة كان من فعل أم كبيرة ، ومنهم من يقول هو مشرك ومنهم من فرق بين الصغيرة وبين الكبيرة ويحكم بكفره وتخليده في النار بارتكابه الكبيرة دون الصغيرة، وحكى عن نجدة الحنفي صاحب النجدات من الخوارج أن صاحب الكبيرة لا يخلد بل يعذب لا محالة بقدر ذنبه ولا يجوز العفو عنه ثم كان عاقبة أمره دخول الجنة، وحكى عن غيلان القدري أنه كان يقول: إن العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة جائراً إلا أنه تعالى إذا عفا عن أحد ارتكب كبيرة لم يجز له تعذيب غيره على تلك الكبيرة ، وحكى عن الحسن البصري كان يقول : إن صاحب الكبيرة منافق.
And the statement of the majority of the Khawarij: Whoever disobeys God has disbelieved, so he is a disbeliever, and his ruling is that he will abide eternally in the Fire, whether the sin was small or large. And some of them say he is a polytheist. And some of them distinguished between the minor sin and the major sin and rule that he is a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire for committing a major sin, but not a minor one. And it is narrated from Najda al-Hanafi, the leader of the Najadat among the Khawarij, that the one who commits a major sin will not abide eternally, but will definitely be punished according to his sin, and forgiveness is not permissible for him, then his final outcome will be entering Paradise. And it is narrated from Ghaylan al-Qadari that he used to say: Forgiveness for the major sinner is permissible, except that when God forgives someone who committed a major sin, it is not permissible for Him to punish another for that same sin. And it is narrated from al-Hasan al-Basri that he used to say: The one who commits a major sin is a hypocrite.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage reports the views of the Khawarij and other early figures on the status of the major sinner. The majority of the Khawarij hold that any act of disobedience to God constitutes disbelief, so the sinner becomes a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire, regardless of whether the sin is small or large. Some Khawarij go further and say the sinner is a polytheist (mushrik). Others among them distinguish between minor and major sins: only the commission of a major sin incurs disbelief and eternal punishment. The passage then mentions three specific divergent views. First, Najda al-Hanafi, leader of the Najadat sub-sect, holds that the major sinner will not abide eternally in Hell but will definitely be punished in proportion to his sin, and forgiveness is not allowed; after punishment, his final outcome is Paradise. Second, Ghaylan al-Qadari holds that forgiveness for the major sinner is permissible, but if God forgives one person for a major sin, He cannot punish another for the same sin. Third, al-Hasan al-Basri is reported to have said that the major sinner is a hypocrite (munafiq).

Key Terms
خوارج
تكفير
شرك
تخليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Khawarij held the same view that any sin leads to eternal punishment.
Correction: The passage shows that some Khawarij distinguished between major and minor sins, and others considered the sinner a polytheist.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of the Khawarij, what is the ruling on a person who commits a minor sin?
A) He is a believer but will be punished temporarily.
B) He is a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire.
C) He is a hypocrite.
D) He is neither a believer nor a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij held that disobedience equals disbelief, but with varying details on the nature of that disbelief and which sins cause it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim commit a sin, do you ever feel a rush of judgment that moves you to write them off? In that moment, are you closer to the Khawarij view than you would like to admit? What would it take for you to instead see them as a sinner in need of mercy, not a disbeliever to be condemned?
?
This lesson shows that even among an extreme group, there was disagreement. How does that humble your own certainty about who is 'in' and who is 'out'? Can you name one person in your life whom you have mentally excommunicated, and commit to seeing them as a Muslim struggling in faith?

Three Other Views on the Major Sinner
Nasafi reports three divergent opinions on the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of Najda al-Hanafi, Ghaylan al-Qadari, and al-Hasan al-Basri on the major sinner, and how they differ from the mainstream Khariji position.

Core Idea
Not all early Muslims agreed on eternal punishment for major sins.

Beyond the Khawarij, several other early figures offered different perspectives on the fate of the major sinner. Nasafi reports three such views, each with its own reasoning. This lesson explores these nuanced positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify each figure and his view.
The passage names Najda al-Hanafi, Ghaylan al-Qadari, and al-Hasan al-Basri. Note the key elements: Najda denies eternal punishment, Ghaylan allows forgiveness with a restriction, and al-Hasan calls the sinner a hypocrite.
2
Compare Najda's view to the Khawarij majority view.
Najda is from the Khawarij subsect Najadat, but he differs: the major sinner is not eternally punished, but will be punished according to the sin and eventually enter Paradise.
This is a significant departure from the majority Khariji stance.
3
Consider the implications of Ghaylan's and al-Hasan's views.
Ghaylan, a Qadari, holds that forgiveness is permissible but restricts God's ability to punish another for the same sin. Al-Hasan says the major sinner is a hypocrite, implying a middle status.
These views reflect early theological debates on iman, kufr, and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The majority of the Khawarij hold that any act of disobedience to God constitutes disbelief, so the sinner becomes a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire, regardless of whether the sin is small or large. ?

?

Sectarian Views on the Major Sinner
Enumerates the views of Khawarij, Najdat, Ghaylan, and Hasan al-Basri on the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse positions of early Islamic sects regarding the fate of the major sinner and the internal differences within the Khawarij.

Core Idea
Even among the Khawarij, no single view on the major sinner's fate exists.

This passage surveys several early Islamic opinions on the fate of a Muslim who commits a major sin. Before we explore the orthodox Sunni position, it is essential to understand the views that were rejected. The passage presents the positions of the Khawarij majority, the Najdat subgroup, Ghaylan al-Qadari, and Hasan al-Basri, showing a spectrum from eternal punishment to eventual paradise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the different views mentioned. Notice the names of groups and individuals.
The passage uses direct quotes from Najda, Ghaylan, and Hasan al-Basri.
2
Identify each group's view
List out the four positions: (1) Majority Khawarij – eternal fire for any sin; (2) Some Khawarij – eternal fire only for major sin; (3) Najdat – punishment then paradise; (4) Ghaylan – forgiveness is permissible but not for others; (5) Hasan al-Basri – major sinner is a hypocrite.
Notice the variation even within the Khawarij.
3
Compare the views on eternal punishment
Ask yourself: which groups believe in eternal punishment (khalid fi al-nar) and which do not? This helps see the spectrum.
The majority Khawarij and those who distinguish major/minor both believe in eternal punishment for major sins; Najdat do not.
4
Consider the theological implications
Reflect on why these positions were considered problematic by mainstream Sunni theologians. Especially the denial of divine forgiveness or the claim that punishment cannot be forgiven.
This passage sets the stage for the Sunni middle path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي الخوارج]: وقول جمهور الخوارج: إن من عصى الله تعالى فقد كفر فكان كافرًا وحكمه أنه يخلد في النار صغيرة كان من فعل أم كبيرة ، ومنهم من يقول هو مشرك ومنهم من فرق بين الصغيرة وبين الكبيرة ويحكم بكفره وتخليده في النار بارتكابه الكبيرة دون الصغيرة، وحكى عن نجدة الحنفي صاحب النجدات من الخوارج أن صاحب الكبيرة لا يخلد بل يعذب لا محالة بقدر ذنبه ولا يجوز العفو عنه ثم كان عاقبة أمره دخول الجنة، وحكى عن غيلان القدري أنه كان يقول: إن العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة جائراً إلا أنه تعالى إذا عفا عن أحد ارتكب كبيرة لم يجز له تعذيب غيره على تلك الكبيرة ، وحكى عن الحسن البصري كان يقول : إن صاحب الكبيرة منافق.
[Opinion of the Khawarij]: The statement of the majority of the Khawarij is that whoever disobeys God Almighty has disbelieved, so he becomes a disbeliever, and his ruling is that he will abide eternally in the Fire, whether the sin he committed was minor or major. Among them are those who say he becomes a polytheist. Among them are those who distinguish between minor and major sins and rule that he disbelieves and will abide eternally in the Fire for committing a major sin, but not a minor sin. And it was related from Najda al-Hanafi, the leader of the Najdat among the Khawarij, that the one who commits a major sin will not abide eternally, but he will certainly be punished according to his sin, and forgiveness for him is not permissible; then his final outcome will be entering Paradise. And it was related from Ghaylan al-Qadari that he used to say: forgiveness for the major sinner is permissible, but if God forgives one who committed a major sin, it is not permissible for Him to punish another for that same sin. And it was related from al-Hasan al-Basri that he used to say: the one who commits a major sin is a hypocrite.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage classifies the positions of several early Islamic sects on the fate of the major sinner. It begins with the majority of the Khawarij, who hold that any disobedience to God constitutes disbelief (كفر), resulting in eternal punishment in Hell, whether the sin is minor or major. A subgroup within the Khawarij says the sinner becomes a polytheist (مشرك). Another subgroup distinguishes between minor and major sins: only major sins lead to disbelief and eternal punishment. Then it mentions Najda al-Hanafi of the Najdat sect, who holds that the major sinner will not be eternally punished but will suffer punishment proportionate to his sin, and forgiveness is not possible; however, his final destination is Paradise. Next is Ghaylan al-Qadari, who says that forgiveness for the major sinner is permissible (جائر) but once God forgives someone for a major sin, He cannot punish another person for the same sin, implying that punishment is particular. Finally, al-Hasan al-Basri (though not a separate sect) is reported to say that the major sinner is a hypocrite (منافق). This classification shows the diversity of opinion on major sin in early Islamic thought and the different approaches to reconciling divine justice, mercy, and the threat of punishment.

Key Terms
الخوارج
النجدات
غيلان القدري
الحسن البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Khawarij hold the same view that any major sinner is an eternal disbeliever.
Correction: The Najdat among the Khawarij held that the major sinner will be punished but eventually enter Paradise, showing internal diversity.

Checkpoint
Which group among the Khawarij held that the major sinner will not abide eternally in Hell but will be punished and then enter Paradise?
A) The majority of Khawarij
B) Najdat under Najda al-Hanafi
C) Ghaylan al-Qadari
D) Hasan al-Basri

Key Takeaway
Early Islamic sects held widely differing views on the fate of the major sinner, from eternal damnation to eventual paradise, which the Sunni tradition later refined into a middle path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely heard that a single major sin could doom a person eternally. But here we learn that Najda al-Hanafi believed the major sinner would eventually enter Paradise. Does this challenge your understanding of God's mercy? How do you reconcile the severity of sin with the possibility of eventual salvation? Will this change how you view others who commit major sins?
?
Hasan al-Basri said the major sinner is a hypocrite. Look at your own life: are there major sins you are committing that make you a hypocrite? What would it mean for you to take this label seriously and repent today, not tomorrow?
?
Ghaylan al-Qadari argued that if God forgives one person for a sin, He cannot punish another for the same sin. Do you sometimes treat God's forgiveness as a guarantee for others, while secretly fearing it won't apply to you? What does this teach you about the combination of God's justice and mercy that you need to personally embrace?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Mu'tazilite Claim of Ijma' on 'Fasiq'
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that the term 'fasiq' is agreed upon by consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi invalidates the Mu'tazilite argument that the term 'fasiq' for major sinners is agreed upon by consensus.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite 'consensus' on 'fasiq' contradicts the earlier consensus that there is no intermediate state between iman and kufr.

The Mu'tazila claimed that calling a major sinner a 'fasiq' was agreed upon by the entire Muslim community (ijma'), while terms like 'believer' and 'unbeliever' were disputed. Al-Nasafi exposes this as false by pointing out that the Mu'tazila themselves violated a prior consensus—the agreement that there is no third category between belief and disbelief. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the opening lines: 'ثم ما تزعم المعتزلة...' until 'كلام باطل'. Note their assertion that the name 'fasiq' is agreed upon.
The claim is selective: they take what they say is agreed and leave the disputed.
2
Identify al-Nasafi's first point
Al-Nasafi asks: what is the real disagreement? Before the Mu'tazila, the ummah had three positions and agreed that anything beyond them is false. By introducing a fourth opinion, the Mu'tazila broke the consensus.
This is a key logical move: showing that the Mu'tazila are actually the ones violating consensus.
3
Understand the second point
There was an explicit consensus that there is no intermediate state (manzila) between iman and kufr. By affirming such an intermediate state, the Mu'tazila oppose the entire ummah.
This is the core of the 'manzila bayna al-manzilatayn' doctrine of the Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Nasafi calls the Mu'tazilite position 'gross ignorance' because it claims consensus while actually contradicting consensus from two angles.
This shows the importance of authentic consensus in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما تزعم المعتزلة أن إطلاق اسم الفسق مجمع عليه وإطلاق ما وراء ذلك من اسم المؤمن والكافر والمنافق مختلف فيه فأخذنا بالمجمع عليه وتركنا المختلف فيه. كلام باطل، إذ هو أخذ بالمجمع عليه وأعرض عن المخالفة، وتحققه ما هي المخالفة؟ فإن الأمة قبل ظهور نحلتهم إذا كانوا على ثلاثة أقوال كانوا مجتمعين على أن ما وراءها قول باطل، فهم إذا أحدثوا قولا رابعا فقد خالفوا الإجماع، وكذا أجمعوا أن لا منزلة بين الإيمان والكفر ، فمن أثبت المنزلة فقد خالف الأمة، والأخذ بالإجماع بما هو مخالف للإجماع من وجهين جهل فاحش. ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت في شيء على أقاويل لم يجز الخروج عن أقاويلهم لما مر أن ذلك منهم إجماع أن ما عدا هذه الأقاويل باطل، فبعد ذلك فالواجب البحث عن الأقاويل وعرضها على الدلائل ليتبين الصحيح من الفاسد والحق من الباطل، وعند العجز عن التمييز بين الحق منها والباطل يجب التوقف والرجوع إلى من أكرم بالعلم والخضوع له والجثو بين يديه ليبين له ذلك، فأما جعل التوقف الذي هو مقتضى تعارض الأدلة ونتيجة العجز عن ترجيح بعض الأقاويل على بعض موجبات الحيرة - مذهبا يتمسك به وعقيدة يدان بها ويناضل عنها ويناظر مخالفوها فيحيد عما توجبه العقول وتقتضيه الأصول.
Then what the Mu'tazila claim — that the application of the name 'fasiq' is agreed upon (mujma' 'alayh) while the application beyond that of the names 'believer', 'unbeliever', and 'hypocrite' is disputed, so we take the agreed-upon and leave the disputed — is false speech. For it is taking the agreed-upon while turning away from disagreement. The reality is: what is the disagreement? The ummah before the emergence of their sect was upon three opinions — they were united that beyond those opinions is false. So when they introduced a fourth opinion, they opposed the consensus. Likewise, they all agreed that there is no intermediate state (manzila) between iman and kufr. So whoever affirms the intermediate state has opposed the ummah. And taking the consensus in a way that is contrary to consensus in two respects is gross ignorance. Then, if the ummah differs on something in several opinions, it is not permissible to go beyond their opinions, because, as mentioned, that constitutes their consensus that what is beyond these opinions is false. After that, the obligation is to examine the opinions and present them to the proofs, so that the correct is distinguished from the corrupt and the truth from falsehood. And when one is unable to distinguish between the truth and falsehood among them, one must pause (tawaqquf) and refer to one who has been honored with knowledge, humility before it, and kneeling before him to clarify it. But making the pause — which is the consequence of conflicting evidences and the result of inability to prefer some opinions over others — a source of bewilderment, as a madhhab to adhere to, a creed to profess, a position to fight for, and a ground to argue against opponents, is to turn away from what the intellects require and what the principles demand.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument that there is a consensus (ijma') on calling the major sinner a fasiq, and that the terms 'believer', 'unbeliever', and 'hypocrite' are disputed. The Mu'tazila claimed that since 'fasiq' is agreed upon, it should be used, and the other terms should be left. Al-Nasafi calls this false. He first points out that the Mu'tazila have turned away from the actual disagreement by selectively taking a supposed consensus. Then he clarifies what 'disagreement' means: before the Mu'tazila appeared, the ummah had three positions (presumably regarding the major sinner's status in the afterlife), and they were united that any position outside these three is false. Thus, when the Mu'tazila introduced a fourth position (the intermediate state or manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), they violated that implicit consensus. Moreover, there was an explicit consensus that there is no intermediate state between iman and kufr. So affirming one is opposing the ummah. Hence, claiming consensus on 'fasiq' while ignoring this is contradictory and ignorant. The author then discusses proper methodology when scholars differ: one cannot simply pick a position based on alleged consensus; rather one must examine all opinions against the proofs (adilla) to distinguish truth from falsehood. If one cannot decide, one should pause (tawaqquf) and seek knowledge from qualified scholars. But turning this bewilderment into a permanent madhhab and creed to defend is misguided, as it abandons reason and principles.

Key Terms
فسق
إجماع
معتزلة
منزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that their consensus on 'fasiq' is valid because the term is commonly used for major sinners in the Quran.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the alleged consensus is invalid because it violates the earlier consensus that there is no intermediate state between belief and disbelief.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw al-Nasafi identifies in the Mu'tazilite argument about consensus on 'fasiq'?
A) The consensus on 'fasiq' is actually disputed among the companions.
B) The Mu'tazila disregarded an earlier consensus that there is no intermediate state between iman and kufr.
C) The Mu'tazila used contradictory hadiths to support their claim.
D) The term 'fasiq' was never used in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim of consensus on 'fasiq' is false because it contradicts the prior consensus that there is no intermediate state between belief and disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, do you tend to latch onto one side that feels 'agreed upon' without checking whether that agreement itself is legitimate? How might you be like the Mu'tazila, claiming consensus while missing the deeper consensus of the early community?
?
Al-Nasafi points out that the Mu'tazila introduced a fourth position despite the ummah agreeing that beyond the three original opinions is false. Have you ever added a new interpretation to a religious issue that actually disrupts a prior implicit agreement among scholars? How will you guard against innovating in matters where the early community had a clear boundary?
?
The Mu'tazila used the method of 'taking the agreed and leaving the disputed' to support their own view. In your own life, when do you selectively apply principles like 'consensus' or 'majority view' to justify your own desires rather than submitting to the full weight of evidence? What would it take for you to truly pause and seek guidance from those with deeper knowledge?

Methodology for Handling Scholarly Disagreement
Al-Nasafi outlines the proper methodology for dealing with differing opinions among scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper method al-Nasafi prescribes when scholars differ: examine opinions against proofs, pause if unable, and consult qualified scholars—not make bewilderment a creed.

Core Idea
True methodology is to seek truth through proofs or qualified scholars, not to adopt confusion as a creed.

After refuting the Mu'tazilite claim of consensus, al-Nasafi outlines the correct approach when scholars have differing opinions. He emphasizes examining all views against the evidence, and if one cannot decide, one must pause and seek guidance from knowledgeable scholars. This lesson explains that methodology and warns against turning uncertainty into a permanent position.

Guided Path
1
Read the methodological section
Read from 'ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت...' to the end. Identify the steps al-Nasafi recommends.
The passage discusses what to do when the ummah has differing opinions.
2
Identify the first two steps
First, one must examine all opinions and present them to the proofs (adilla) to distinguish truth from falsehood. This is the ideal: using reason and evidence.
Proofs here refer to rational and scriptural arguments.
3
Understand the fallback
If one is unable to distinguish, one must pause (tawaqquf) and refer to a scholar who has knowledge and humility.
This pause is not a permanent position but a temporary measure until clarity is gained.
4
Recognize the error to avoid
Al-Nasafi criticizes those who make this pause a madhhab and creed, fighting for it. This turns uncertainty into a dogma, which is against reason and principles.
Bewilderment (hayra) is a result of conflicting evidence, not a valid theological stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما تزعم المعتزلة أن إطلاق اسم الفسق مجمع عليه وإطلاق ما وراء ذلك من اسم المؤمن والكافر والمنافق مختلف فيه فأخذنا بالمجمع عليه وتركنا المختلف فيه. كلام باطل، إذ هو أخذ بالمجمع عليه وأعرض عن المخالفة، وتحققه ما هي المخالفة؟ فإن الأمة قبل ظهور نحلتهم إذا كانوا على ثلاثة أقوال كانوا مجتمعين على أن ما وراءها قول باطل، فهم إذا أحدثوا قولا رابعا فقد خالفوا الإجماع، وكذا أجمعوا أن لا منزلة بين الإيمان والكفر ، فمن أثبت المنزلة فقد خالف الأمة، والأخذ بالإجماع بما هو مخالف للإجماع من وجهين جهل فاحش. ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت في شيء على أقاويل لم يجز الخروج عن أقاويلهم لما مر أن ذلك منهم إجماع أن ما عدا هذه الأقاويل باطل، فبعد ذلك فالواجب البحث عن الأقاويل وعرضها على الدلائل ليتبين الصحيح من الفاسد والحق من الباطل، وعند العجز عن التمييز بين الحق منها والباطل يجب التوقف والرجوع إلى من أكرم بالعلم والخضوع له والجثو بين يديه ليبين له ذلك، فأما جعل التوقف الذي هو مقتضى تعارض الأدلة ونتيجة العجز عن ترجيح بعض الأقاويل على بعض موجبات الحيرة - مذهبا يتمسك به وعقيدة يدان بها ويناضل عنها ويناظر مخالفوها فيحيد عما توجبه العقول وتقتضيه الأصول.

The ummah before the emergence of their sect was upon three opinions — they were united that beyond those opinions is false. So when they introduced a fourth opinion, they opposed the consensus. Likewise, they all agreed that there is no intermediate state (manzila) between iman and kufr. So whoever affirms the intermediate state has opposed the ummah. And taking the consensus in a way that is contrary to consensus in two respects is gross ignorance. After that, the obligation is to examine the opinions and present them to the proofs, so that the correct is distinguished from the corrupt and the truth from falsehood. And when one is unable to distinguish between the truth and falsehood among them, one must pause (tawaqquf) and refer to one who has been honored with knowledge, humility before it, and kneeling before him to clarify it. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When scholars disagree, one is allowed to choose any opinion or remain in perpetual confusion without seeking guidance.
Correction: Al-Nasafi prescribes a clear method: examine proofs, and if unable, pause and consult a scholar, not adopt bewilderment as a creed.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what should a person do if they are unable to distinguish truth from falsehood among differing scholarly opinions?
A) Adopt the most popular opinion.
B) Pause and consult a qualified scholar.
C) Choose the opinion that seems easiest.
D) Reject all opinions and follow personal reason.

Key Takeaway
Proper methodology when scholars differ: examine opinions with proofs, pause if unable, and consult qualified scholars, but never turn bewilderment into a creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement among scholars, do you honestly search for the proof or do you take the easier path of following what feels comfortable? What would it cost you to truly submit to the truth?
?
Have you ever made uncertainty into a safe position, defending your doubt as if it were a virtue? How can you humble yourself before Allah and His guidance instead of clinging to confusion?
?
Are you quick to consult a knowledgeable scholar when you are unable to decide, or do you let pride prevent you from seeking help? What does your hesitation reveal about your heart's attachment to your own opinion?

The Problem of Generality in Verses of Promise and Threat
Al-Nasafi sets up the issue of generality in verses of promise and threat and critiques the Mu'tazili accusation against Ahl al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why applying all verses of promise and threat generally leads to contradiction, and how different sects prioritized either promise or threat verses.

Core Idea
Generalizing all verses of promise and threat would contradict God's wisdom.

Al-Nasafi begins by refuting the Mu'tazili accusation that allowing forgiveness for major sins constitutes a breach of promise. He then highlights a crucial problem: both promise and threat verses appear in general form, but taking them all literally leads to contradiction. This sets the stage for the different sectarian approaches to resolving the tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening accusation
Read the first part where Al-Nasafi describes how the Mu'tazila accuse Ahl al-Haqq of breaking God's promise by allowing forgiveness. Note his strong refutation.
This shows the importance of the topic.
2
Identify the problem of generality
Next, notice that both promise and threat verses came in general form. Al-Nasafi argues that applying all of them generally would cause contradiction, which is unwise.
This is the logical starting point for the Maturidi approach.
3
Compare the two sectarian positions
Al-Nasafi then presents the views of the Mu'tazila/Khawarij, who prioritize threat verses for deterrence, and the Murji'a, who prioritize promise verses based on God's mercy. Consider the strengths and weaknesses of each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آيات الوعد والوعيد والكلام فيها] ثم هم ينسبون أهل الحق في قولهم بجواز العفو عن الكبيرة إلى إثبات الخلف في الوعيد ويشنعون عليهم بذلك وهذا من أعظم الوقاحات. ثم وردت آيات في الوعد خارجة على صيغة العموم وكذا آيات الوعيد، ولا وجه إلى إجراء الكل على العموم لما فيه من إثبات التناقض في آيات الله تعالى والتعارض في أدلته، وذلك خارج عن الحكمة. ثم بعد ذلك اضطربت الأقاويل فزعمت المعتزلة والخوارج (۱) أن (۲) آيات الوعيد أحق بالعموم لما هو أبلغ في الزجر، وزعمت المرجئة أن آيات الوعد أحق بالعموم لأنه أحق بالذي عرف من صفات الله تعالى من (۳) الرحمة والعفو والغفران. والأصل عندنا أن ما كان من الآيات الواردة في الوعيد مقرونا بذكر الخلود فهو من المستحلّين لذلك، لما أنهم كفروا لاستحلال ذلك فأوعدوا على كفرهم في الحقيقة وذكر تلك الجريمة لكونها سببًا للكفر وطريقاً إليه، ولهذا قلنا في تأويل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا ) (٤) أي متعمدًا الإماتة أي قتله لأجل أنه مؤمن ومن هذا قصده في القتل يكون كافرًا ألا ترى أنه حيث ذكر (٩) قتل العمد لا على الاستحلال فمن باب إيجاب القصاص بقي اسم الإيمان والأخوة بينه وبين المؤمنين وجعله أملاً للرحمة والتخفيف على ما بينا، فكان ذلك دليلاً أن هذه الآية وردت في المستحل وما يزعمون أن هذا وعيد القتل لا
[Verses of Promise and Threat and Discussion Regarding Them] Then they (the Mu'tazila) attribute to the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) in their saying about the permissibility of forgiveness for major sins the establishment of breach of promise in the threat, and they revile them for that, and this is among the greatest impudences. Then verses came in promise (al-wa'd) in the form of generality, and likewise verses of threat (al-wa'id). And there is no way to apply all of them to the general meaning because that would entail establishing contradiction in the verses of God Almighty and conflict in His proofs, and that is outside of wisdom. Then after that the opinions differed: the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij (1) claimed that the verses of threat are more deserving of being general because they are more emphatic in deterrence, and the Murji'a claimed that the verses of promise are more deserving of being general because they are more consistent with what is known of God's attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness (3). And the principle with us is that whatever verses are revealed about threat accompanied by mention of eternity are about those who deem (the sin) permissible (mustahillun), because they disbelieved due to that deeming permissible, so they were threatened for their disbelief in reality, and that crime was mentioned because it is a cause for disbelief and a path to it. And for this we said in the interpretation of His statement, exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein' (4), meaning intentionally causing death, i.e., killing him because he is a believer, and whoever has this intent in killing becomes a disbeliever. Do you not see that where He mentioned intentional murder without deeming it permissible, it is under the ruling of obligatory retaliation (qisas), the name of faith and brotherhood remained between him and the believers, and He made him hopeful for mercy and alleviation (9) as we explained? So that was evidence that this verse was revealed regarding the one who deems (killing) permissible, and what they claim that this is a threat for murder without [further specification] is not [correct].
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the Maturidi principle for interpreting verses of eternal punishment. Al-Nasafi begins by accusing the Mu'tazila of attributing to Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., the Ash'ari/Maturidi school) the claim that they establish khulf (breach of promise) in the threat by allowing forgiveness for major sins, and says this accusation is impudent. Then he notes that both verses of promise and threat came in general form, but applying all of them generally would lead to contradiction, which is not wise. After that, he mentions the differing positions: the Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue that verses of threat should be taken generally because they are more effective in deterrence, while the Murji'a argue that verses of promise should be taken generally because they align with God's attributes of mercy. Then he states the Maturidi principle: verses of eternal punishment that mention eternity refer to those who deem the sin permissible (mustahillun), because they disbelieve by deeming it permissible, so the threat is actually for their disbelief, and the sin is mentioned as a cause. He applies this to the verse about killing a believer intentionally: it means killing because of his faith, which constitutes apostasy. Evidence is that when intentional murder is mentioned without deeming it permissible, it falls under qisas and the killer is still considered a believer, with hope for mercy. Thus, the verse targets the mustahill. The passage ends abruptly, cutting off a refutation of those who claim this verse is a general threat for any intentional killing.

Key Terms
الوعد
الوعيد
العموم
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why can't all verses of promise and threat be applied generally?
A) Because they contradict each other.
B) Because they are abrogated.
C) Because they are metaphorical.
D) Because we don't know their meaning.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of verses of promise and threat requires a principled approach to avoid contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Qur'anic verses of threat, do you feel a sense of hopeless fear, or do you balance them with verses of mercy? Which verses do you tend to emphasize more? This lesson challenges you to hold both in harmony. Today, commit to reading one promise verse and one threat verse together and reflecting on how they complement each other.
?
Al-Nasafi calls the Mu'tazili accusation 'impudent.' Have you ever dismissed a fellow Muslim's belief as 'obviously wrong' without first trying to understand their reasoning from the full Qur'an? Ask yourself: are you more loyal to your school of thought or to the pursuit of truth through comprehensive evidence? Resolve to listen carefully to a view you disagree with this week.
?
This lesson reveals that even the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who seem strict, had a principle: they prioritized deterrence. And the Murji'a prioritized mercy. What is your own default principle when interpreting difficult verses? Is it fear of punishment, hope for mercy, or something else? If you haven't consciously developed a balanced approach, what step will you take today to learn the Maturidi method of interpretation?
